## Education

Main article: [Education in California](/wiki/Education_in_California "Education in California")

See also: [Spanish bilingual education in California](/wiki/Spanish_bilingual_education_in_California "Spanish bilingual education in California")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Santa_Barbara_Senior_High_School_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Santa_Barbara_Senior_High_School_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Santa_Barbara_Senior_High_School_(cropped).jpg)

[Santa Barbara High School](/wiki/Santa_Barbara_High_School "Santa Barbara High School"), one of the oldest high schools in continuous use in Southern California

California has the most school students in the country, with over 6.2 million in the 2005–06 school year, giving California more students in school than 36 states have in total population and one of the highest projected enrollments in the country.[[267]](#cite_note-274)
Public [secondary education](/wiki/Secondary_education_in_the_United_States "Secondary education in the United States") consists of [high schools](/wiki/High_schools_in_California "High schools in California") that teach elective courses in trades, languages, and liberal arts with tracks for gifted, college-bound and industrial arts students. California's public educational system is supported by a [unique constitutional amendment](/wiki/California_Proposition_98_(1988) "California Proposition 98 (1988)") that requires a minimum annual funding level for grades K–12 and [community colleges](/wiki/List_of_California_Community_Colleges "List of California Community Colleges") that grows with the economy and student enrollment figures.[[268]](#cite_note-Prop_98-275)

In 2016, California's K–12 public school per-pupil spending was ranked 22nd in the nation ($11,500 per student vs. $11,800 for the U.S. average).[[269]](#cite_note-Governing_State_Ed_data-276)

For 2012, California's K–12 public schools ranked 48th in the number of employees per student, at 0.102 (the U.S. average was 0.137), while paying the 7th most per employee, $49,000 (the U.S. average was $39,000).[[270]](#cite_note-Urban_study-277)[[271]](#cite_note-Urban_study_notes-278)[[272]](#cite_note-MN_state_comparison-279)

### Higher education

Main article: [List of colleges and universities in California](/wiki/List_of_colleges_and_universities_in_California "List of colleges and universities in California")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Berkeley_glade_afternoon.jpg/250px-Berkeley_glade_afternoon.jpg)](/wiki/File:Berkeley_glade_afternoon.jpg)

[UC Berkeley](/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley "University of California, Berkeley") is the oldest campus of the [University of California](/wiki/University_of_California "University of California"), and the state's flagship public university.

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Stanford_University_Main_Quad_-_7_June_2009.jpg/250px-Stanford_University_Main_Quad_-_7_June_2009.jpg)](/wiki/File:Stanford_University_Main_Quad_-_7_June_2009.jpg)

[Stanford University](/wiki/Stanford_University "Stanford University") is a private university that is one of the top-ranked universities in the world.[[273]](#cite_note-280)

California public [postsecondary education](/wiki/Postsecondary_education "Postsecondary education") is organized into three separate systems:

* The state's [public](/wiki/Public_university "Public university") [research university](/wiki/Research_university "Research university") [system](/wiki/State_university_system "State university system") is the [University of California](/wiki/University_of_California "University of California") (UC). As of fall 2011, the University of California had a combined [student](/wiki/Student "Student") body of 234,464 students.[[274]](#cite_note-About-281) There are ten UC campuses; nine are general campuses offering both undergraduate and graduate programs which culminate in the award of bachelor's degrees, master's degrees, and doctorates; there is one specialized campus, [UC San Francisco](/wiki/UC_San_Francisco "UC San Francisco"), which is entirely dedicated to graduate education in [health care](/wiki/Health_care_in_California "Health care in California"), and is home to the [UCSF Medical Center](/wiki/UCSF_Medical_Center "UCSF Medical Center"), the highest-ranked [hospital in California](/wiki/Hospitals_in_California "Hospitals in California").[[275]](#cite_note-282) The system was originally intended to accept the top one-eighth of California high school students, but several of the campuses have become even more selective.[[276]](#cite_note-283)[[277]](#cite_note-284)[[278]](#cite_note-Powell-285) The UC system historically held exclusive authority to award the doctorate, but this has since changed and CSU now has limited statutory authorization to award a handful of types of doctoral degrees independently of UC.
* The [California State University](/wiki/California_State_University "California State University") (CSU) system has almost 430,000 students. The CSU (which takes the definite article in its abbreviated form, while UC does not) was originally intended to accept the top one-third of California high school students, but several of the campuses have become much more selective.[[278]](#cite_note-Powell-285)[[279]](#cite_note-286) The CSU was originally authorized to award only bachelor's and master's degrees, and could award the doctorate only as part of joint programs with UC or private universities. Since then, CSU has been granted the authority to independently award several doctoral degrees (in specific academic fields that do not intrude upon UC's traditional jurisdiction).
* The [California Community Colleges](/wiki/California_Community_Colleges "California Community Colleges") system provides lower-division coursework culminating in the associate degree, as well as basic skills and workforce training culminating in various kinds of certificates. (Fifteen California community colleges now award four-year bachelor's degrees in disciplines which are in high demand in their geographical area.[[280]](#cite_note-287)) It is the largest network of higher education in the U.S., composed of 112 colleges serving a student population of over 2.6 million.

California is also home to notable private universities such as [Stanford University](/wiki/Stanford_University "Stanford University"), the [California Institute of Technology](/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology "California Institute of Technology") (Caltech), the [University of Southern California](/wiki/University_of_Southern_California "University of Southern California"), the [Claremont Colleges](/wiki/Claremont_Colleges "Claremont Colleges"), [Santa Clara University](/wiki/Santa_Clara_University "Santa Clara University"), [Loyola Marymount University](/wiki/Loyola_Marymount_University "Loyola Marymount University"), the [University of San Diego](/wiki/University_of_San_Diego "University of San Diego"), the [University of San Francisco](/wiki/University_of_San_Francisco "University of San Francisco"), [Chapman University](/wiki/Chapman_University "Chapman University"), [Pepperdine University](/wiki/Pepperdine_University "Pepperdine University"), [Occidental College](/wiki/Occidental_College "Occidental College"), and [University of the Pacific](/wiki/University_of_the_Pacific_(United_States) "University of the Pacific (United States)"), among numerous other private colleges and universities, including many religious and special-purpose institutions. California has a particularly high density of arts colleges, including the [California College of the Arts](/wiki/California_College_of_the_Arts "California College of the Arts"), [California Institute of the Arts](/wiki/California_Institute_of_the_Arts "California Institute of the Arts"), [San Francisco Art Institute](/wiki/San_Francisco_Art_Institute "San Francisco Art Institute"), [Art Center College of Design](/wiki/Art_Center_College_of_Design "Art Center College of Design"), and [Academy of Art University](/wiki/Academy_of_Art_University "Academy of Art University"), among others.

## Economy

Main article: [Economy of California](/wiki/Economy_of_California "Economy of California")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/SJ_skyline_at_night_horizontal.jpg/330px-SJ_skyline_at_night_horizontal.jpg)](/wiki/File:SJ_skyline_at_night_horizontal.jpg)

[Silicon Valley](/wiki/Silicon_Valley "Silicon Valley") is the largest [tech hub](/wiki/List_of_technology_centers "List of technology centers") in the world and home to [Big Tech](/wiki/Big_Tech "Big Tech") companies like [Apple](/wiki/Apple_Inc. "Apple Inc."), [Alphabet](/wiki/Alphabet_Inc. "Alphabet Inc."), [Meta](/wiki/Meta_Platforms,_Inc. "Meta Platforms, Inc."), [Nvidia](/wiki/Nvidia_Corporation "Nvidia Corporation"), [Intel](/wiki/Intel_Corporation "Intel Corporation"), [HP](/wiki/HP_Inc. "HP Inc."), [Netflix, Inc.](/wiki/Netflix,_Inc. "Netflix, Inc."), [Uber](/wiki/Uber_Technologies,_Inc. "Uber Technologies, Inc."), and many more.

California's economy ranks among the largest in the world. As of 2024[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=California&action=edit), the [gross state product](/wiki/Gross_state_product "Gross state product") (GSP) is $4.172 [trillion](/wiki/1000000000000_(number) "1000000000000 (number)") ($102,500 per capita), the [largest of any U.S. state](/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_GDP "List of U.S. states and territories by GDP").[[14]](#cite_note-GDPByState-16) California is responsible for one seventh of the nation's gross domestic product (GDP).[[281]](#cite_note-gdp-288) As of 2025[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=California&action=edit), California's nominal GDP is larger than all but four countries.[[15]](#cite_note-:1-17) In terms of [purchasing power parity](/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity "Purchasing power parity") (PPP),[[282]](#cite_note-8large-289) it is larger than all but eight countries.[[283]](#cite_note-worldbank2012-290) California's economy is larger than [Africa](/wiki/Economy_of_Africa "Economy of Africa") and [Australia](/wiki/Economy_of_Australia "Economy of Australia") and is almost as large as [South America](/wiki/Economy_of_South_America "Economy of South America").[[284]](#cite_note-291) The state recorded total, non-farm employment of 16,677,800[[285]](#cite_note-bls.gov-292) as of September 2021[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=California&action=edit) among 966,224 employer establishments.[[286]](#cite_note-293) As of 2024, California is home to 57 of the [*Fortune* 500](/wiki/Fortune_500 "Fortune 500") companies, the [highest number of any U.S. state](/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_number_of_Fortune_500_companies "List of U.S. states by number of Fortune 500 companies").[[287]](#cite_note-294)

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/The_port_of_Long_Beach_2_by_Don_Ramey_Logan.jpg/250px-The_port_of_Long_Beach_2_by_Don_Ramey_Logan.jpg)](/wiki/File:The_port_of_Long_Beach_2_by_Don_Ramey_Logan.jpg)

The combined [Port of Los Angeles](/wiki/Port_of_Los_Angeles "Port of Los Angeles")-[Port of Long Beach](/wiki/Port_of_Long_Beach "Port of Long Beach") is the largest port in the U.S. by import volume and one of the [busiest ports in the world](/wiki/List_of_busiest_container_ports "List of busiest container ports").

As the largest and second-largest U.S. ports respectively, the [Port of Los Angeles](/wiki/Port_of_Los_Angeles "Port of Los Angeles") and the [Port of Long Beach](/wiki/Port_of_Long_Beach "Port of Long Beach") in Southern California collectively play a pivotal role in the global supply chain, together hauling in about 40% of all imports to the U.S. by [TEU](/wiki/Twenty-foot_equivalent_units "Twenty-foot equivalent units") volume.[[21]](#cite_note-Karlamangla-23) The [Port of Oakland](/wiki/Port_of_Oakland "Port of Oakland") and [Port of Hueneme](/wiki/Port_of_Hueneme "Port of Hueneme") are the 10th and 26th largest seaports in the U.S., respectively, by number of TEUs handled.[[288]](#cite_note-Burnson-295)

The five largest sectors of employment in California are trade, transportation, and utilities; government; professional and business services; education and health services; and leisure and hospitality. In output, the five largest sectors are financial services, followed by trade, transportation, and utilities; education and health services; government; and manufacturing.[[289]](#cite_note-296) California has an [unemployment](/wiki/California_unemployment_statistics "California unemployment statistics") rate of 3.9% as of September 2022[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=California&action=edit).[[285]](#cite_note-bls.gov-292)

California's economy is dependent on trade and international related commerce accounts for about one-quarter of the state's economy, and representing 7% of their GDP; California's biggest trade partner is Mexico. In 2008, California exported $144 billion worth of goods, up from $134 billion in 2007 and $127 billion in 2006.[[290]](#cite_note-Cal_Trade_Statistics_2008-297) Vehicles, computers and electronic products are California's top exports, accounting for 42% of all the state's exports in 2008; over 50 car companies operate in California, such as [Tesla](/wiki/Tesla,_Inc. "Tesla, Inc.") and [Mazda](/wiki/Mazda "Mazda").[[290]](#cite_note-Cal_Trade_Statistics_2008-297)

### Agriculture

Main article: [Agriculture in California](/wiki/Agriculture_in_California "Agriculture in California")

Further information: [Strawberry cultivation in California](/wiki/Strawberry_cultivation_in_California "Strawberry cultivation in California"), [Production of peaches in California](/wiki/Production_of_peaches_in_California "Production of peaches in California"), and [Walnuts in California](/wiki/Walnuts_in_California "Walnuts in California")

See also: [California nut crimes](/wiki/California_nut_crimes "California nut crimes")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Autumn_in_Napa_Valley_vineyards.jpg/250px-Autumn_in_Napa_Valley_vineyards.jpg)](/wiki/File:Autumn_in_Napa_Valley_vineyards.jpg)

[California vineyards](/wiki/California_wine "California wine") in [Wine Country](/wiki/Wine_Country "Wine Country"). The [agricultural industry in California](/wiki/Agriculture_in_California "Agriculture in California") is the largest [in the U.S.](/wiki/Agriculture_in_the_United_States "Agriculture in the United States")

Agriculture is an important sector in California's economy. According to the [U.S. Department of Agriculture](/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Agriculture "U.S. Department of Agriculture"), in 2011, the three largest California agricultural products by value were [milk and cream](/wiki/California_dairy_industry "California dairy industry"), shelled [almonds](/wiki/Almond_cultivation_in_California "Almond cultivation in California"), and [grapes](/wiki/Grape_cultivation_in_California "Grape cultivation in California").[[20]](#cite_note-Jason_Gibson-2012-22) Farming-related sales more than quadrupled over the past three decades, from $7.3 billion in 1974 to nearly $31 billion in 2004.[[291]](#cite_note-Cal_Facts_2006_State_Economy-298) This increase has occurred despite a 15% decline in acreage devoted to farming during the period, and water supply suffering from chronic instability. Factors contributing to the growth in sales-per-acre include more intensive use of active farmlands and technological improvements in crop production.[[291]](#cite_note-Cal_Facts_2006_State_Economy-298) In 2008, California's 81,500 farms and ranches generated $36.2 billion products revenue.[[292]](#cite_note-California_Agricultural_Production_Statistics-299) In 2011, that number grew to $43.5 billion products revenue.[[292]](#cite_note-California_Agricultural_Production_Statistics-299) The agriculture sector accounts for 2% of the state's GDP and employs around 3% of its total workforce.[[293]](#cite_note-300)

### Income

See also: [California locations by per capita income](/wiki/California_locations_by_per_capita_income "California locations by per capita income")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Sleeping_Beauty_Castle_2019.jpg/250px-Sleeping_Beauty_Castle_2019.jpg)](/wiki/File:Sleeping_Beauty_Castle_2019.jpg)

California is the most visited state in the country.[[294]](#cite_note-301) [Disneyland](/wiki/Disneyland "Disneyland") in [Anaheim](/wiki/Anaheim "Anaheim") is a major tourist destination, with 16.9 million annual visits in 2022.[[295]](#cite_note-302)

[Per capita GDP](/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_GDP_per_capita "List of U.S. states by GDP per capita") in 2021 was $85,546, ranking fourth in the nation. [Per capita income](/wiki/California_locations_by_per_capita_income "California locations by per capita income") varies widely by geographic region and profession.[[296]](#cite_note-BEA_State_Personal_Income_2006-303) The Central Valley is the most impoverished, with [migrant farm workers](/wiki/Migrant_worker "Migrant worker") making less than [minimum wage](/wiki/Minimum_wage "Minimum wage"). According to a 2005 report by the [Congressional Research Service](/wiki/Congressional_Research_Service "Congressional Research Service"), the [San Joaquin Valley](/wiki/San_Joaquin_Valley "San Joaquin Valley") was characterized as one of the most economically depressed regions in the U.S., on par with the region of [Appalachia](/wiki/Appalachia "Appalachia").[[297]](#cite_note-CRS_San_Joaquin_Valley-304)

Using the supplemental poverty measure, California has a [poverty rate](/wiki/Poverty_in_the_United_States "Poverty in the United States") of 23.5%, the highest of any state in the country.[[298]](#cite_note-305) However, using the official measure the poverty rate was only 13.3% as of 2017.[[299]](#cite_note-306) Many coastal cities include some of the wealthiest per-capita areas in the U.S. The high-technology sectors in Northern California, specifically [Silicon Valley](/wiki/Silicon_Valley "Silicon Valley"), in [Santa Clara](/wiki/Santa_Clara_County,_California "Santa Clara County, California") and [San Mateo counties](/wiki/San_Mateo_County,_California "San Mateo County, California"), have emerged from the economic downturn caused by the [dot-com bust](/wiki/Dot-com_bubble "Dot-com bubble").

In 2019, there were 1,042,027 millionaire households in the state, more than any other state in the nation.[[300]](#cite_note-307) In 2010, California residents were ranked first among the states with the best average credit score of 754.[[301]](#cite_note-308)

* [![If California was an independent country, its gross domestic product (nominal) would rank fifth in the world (2022).[302]](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/USA-World_Nominal_GDP.PNG/500px-USA-World_Nominal_GDP.PNG)](/wiki/File:USA-World_Nominal_GDP.PNG "If California was an independent country, its gross domestic product (nominal) would rank fifth in the world (2022).[302]")

  If California was an independent country, its gross domestic product (nominal) would rank [fifth in the world](/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_GDP "List of U.S. states and territories by GDP") (2022).[[302]](#cite_note-309)
* [![California counties by GDP (2021)[303]](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/California_counties_by_GDP_2021.png/330px-California_counties_by_GDP_2021.png)](/wiki/File:California_counties_by_GDP_2021.png "California counties by GDP (2021)[303]")

  California counties by GDP (2021)[[303]](#cite_note-310)

### State finances

Main articles: [California state finances](/wiki/California_state_finances "California state finances") and [2008–12 California budget crisis](/wiki/2008%E2%80%9312_California_budget_crisis "2008–12 California budget crisis")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/California_economic_regions_map_%28labeled_and_colored%29.svg/250px-California_economic_regions_map_%28labeled_and_colored%29.svg.png)](/wiki/File:California_economic_regions_map_(labeled_and_colored).svg)

[California economic regions](/wiki/Economic_regions_of_California "Economic regions of California")

State spending increased from $56 billion in 1998 to $127 billion in 2011.[[304]](#cite_note-311)[[305]](#cite_note-312) California has the third highest per capita spending on welfare among the states, as well as the highest spending on welfare at $6.67 billion.[[306]](#cite_note-313) In January 2011, California's total debt was at least $265 billion.[[307]](#cite_note-314) On June 27, 2013, Governor Jerry Brown signed a balanced budget (no deficit) for the state, its first in decades; however, the state's debt remains at $132 billion.[[308]](#cite_note-315)[[309]](#cite_note-316)

With the passage of [Proposition 30 in 2012](/wiki/California_Proposition_30_(2012) "California Proposition 30 (2012)") and [Proposition 55 in 2016](/wiki/2016_California_Proposition_55 "2016 California Proposition 55"), California now levies a 13.3% maximum marginal [income tax](/wiki/Income_tax "Income tax") rate with ten [tax brackets](/wiki/Tax_bracket "Tax bracket"), ranging from 1% at the bottom tax bracket of $0 annual individual income to 13.3% for annual individual income over $1,000,000 (though the top brackets are only temporary until Proposition 55 expires at the end of 2030). While Proposition 30 also enacted a minimum [state sales tax](/wiki/Sales_taxes_in_the_United_States#California "Sales taxes in the United States") of 7.5%, this sales tax increase was not extended by Proposition 55 and reverted to a previous minimum state sales tax rate of 7.25% in 2017. Local governments can and do levy additional sales taxes in addition to this minimum rate.[[310]](#cite_note-317)

All [real property](/wiki/Real_property "Real property") is taxable annually. Property tax increases are capped at 2% annually or the rate of inflation (whichever is lower), per [Proposition 13](/wiki/California_Proposition_13_(1978) "California Proposition 13 (1978)").

### Energy

Main article: [Energy in California](/wiki/Energy_in_California "Energy in California")

Further information: [Solar power in California](/wiki/Solar_power_in_California "Solar power in California") and [Wind power in California](/wiki/Wind_power_in_California "Wind power in California")

See also: [History of oil in California through 1930](/wiki/History_of_oil_in_California_through_1930 "History of oil in California through 1930") and [Plug-in electric vehicles in California](/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicles_in_California "Plug-in electric vehicles in California")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Moss_Landing_Power_Plant_p1270026.jpg/250px-Moss_Landing_Power_Plant_p1270026.jpg)](/wiki/File:Moss_Landing_Power_Plant_p1270026.jpg)

[Moss Landing Power Plant](/wiki/Moss_Landing_Power_Plant "Moss Landing Power Plant"), located on the coast of [Monterey Bay](/wiki/Monterey_Bay "Monterey Bay")

Because it is the most populous state in the U.S., California is one of the country's largest users of energy. The state has extensive hydro-electric energy generation facilities, however, moving water is the single largest energy use in the state. Also, due to high energy rates, conservation mandates, mild weather in the largest population centers and strong [environmental movement](/wiki/Environmental_movement "Environmental movement"), its *per capita* energy use is one of the smallest of any state in the U.S.[[311]](#cite_note-318) Due to the high electricity demand, California imports more electricity than any other state, primarily hydroelectric power from states in the Pacific Northwest (via [Path 15](/wiki/Path_15 "Path 15") and [Path 66](/wiki/Path_66 "Path 66")) and coal- and natural gas-fired production from the desert Southwest via [Path 46](/wiki/Path_46 "Path 46").[[312]](#cite_note-319)

The [state's crude oil and natural gas deposits](/wiki/California_oil_and_gas_industry "California oil and gas industry") are located in the Central Valley and [along the coast](/wiki/Offshore_oil_and_gas_in_California "Offshore oil and gas in California"), including the large [Midway-Sunset Oil Field](/wiki/Midway-Sunset_Oil_Field "Midway-Sunset Oil Field"). Natural gas-fired [power plants](/wiki/List_of_power_stations_in_California "List of power stations in California") typically account for more than one-half of state electricity generation.

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Ho_000155_170593_515241_4578_%2835974057893%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Ho_000155_170593_515241_4578_%2835974057893%29_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Ho_000155_170593_515241_4578_(35974057893)_(cropped).jpg)

[Ivanpah Solar Power Facility](/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility "Ivanpah Solar Power Facility"), located in the [Mojave Desert](/wiki/Mojave_Desert "Mojave Desert")

As a result of the state's strong environmental movement, California has some of the most aggressive [renewable energy](/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_California "Renewable energy in California") goals in the U.S. The Clean Energy, Jobs and Affordability Act of 2022 commits the state to running its operations on clean, renewable energy resources by 2035, and SB 1203 also requires the state to achieve net-zero operations for all agencies. Currently, several [solar power plants](/wiki/Solar_power_in_California "Solar power in California") such as the [Solar Energy Generating Systems](/wiki/Solar_Energy_Generating_Systems "Solar Energy Generating Systems") facility are located in the [Mojave Desert](/wiki/Mojave_Desert "Mojave Desert"). [California's wind farms](/wiki/Wind_power_in_California "Wind power in California") include [Altamont Pass](/wiki/Altamont_Pass_Wind_Farm "Altamont Pass Wind Farm"), [San Gorgonio Pass](/wiki/San_Gorgonio_Pass_Wind_Farm "San Gorgonio Pass Wind Farm"), and [Tehachapi Pass](/wiki/Tehachapi_Pass_Wind_Farm "Tehachapi Pass Wind Farm"). The Tehachapi area is also where the [Tehachapi Energy Storage Project](/wiki/Tehachapi_Energy_Storage_Project "Tehachapi Energy Storage Project") is located.[[313]](#cite_note-International2-320) Several dams across the state provide [hydro-electric power](/wiki/Hydroelectricity "Hydroelectricity"). It would be possible to convert the total supply to 100% renewable energy, including heating, cooling and mobility, by 2050.[[314]](#cite_note-321)

California has one major [nuclear power](/wiki/Nuclear_power "Nuclear power") plant ([Diablo Canyon](/wiki/Diablo_Canyon_Power_Plant "Diablo Canyon Power Plant")) in operation. The [San Onofre nuclear plant](/wiki/San_Onofre_Nuclear_Generating_Station "San Onofre Nuclear Generating Station") was shut down in 2013. More than 1,700 tons of [radioactive waste](/wiki/Radioactive_waste "Radioactive waste") are stored at San Onofre,[[315]](#cite_note-322) and sit on the coast where there is a record of past [tsunamis](/wiki/Tsunami "Tsunami").[[316]](#cite_note-323) Voters [banned the approval of new nuclear power plants](/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement_in_California "Anti-nuclear movement in California") since the late 1970s because of concerns over [radioactive waste disposal](/wiki/High-level_radioactive_waste_management "High-level radioactive waste management").[[317]](#cite_note-Doyle_2009-324)[[note 8]](#cite_note-326) Several cities such as Oakland, [Berkeley](/wiki/Berkeley,_California "Berkeley, California") and [Davis](/wiki/Davis,_California "Davis, California") have declared themselves as [nuclear-free zones](/wiki/Nuclear-free_zone "Nuclear-free zone").

### Transportation

Main article: [Transportation in California](/wiki/Transportation_in_California "Transportation in California")

See also: [History of rail transportation in California](/wiki/History_of_rail_transportation_in_California "History of rail transportation in California")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/GoldenGateBridge-001.jpg/250px-GoldenGateBridge-001.jpg)](/wiki/File:GoldenGateBridge-001.jpg)

The [Golden Gate Bridge](/wiki/Golden_Gate_Bridge "Golden Gate Bridge") in the [San Francisco Bay Area](/wiki/San_Francisco_Bay_Area "San Francisco Bay Area"), is one of the most famous bridges in the world.

#### Highways

California's vast terrain is connected by [an extensive system](/wiki/California_Freeway_and_Expressway_System "California Freeway and Expressway System") of [controlled-access highways](/wiki/Controlled-access_highways "Controlled-access highways") ('freeways'), [limited-access roads](/wiki/Limited-access_road "Limited-access road") ('expressways'), and [highways](/wiki/State_highways_in_California "State highways in California"). California is known for its [car culture](/wiki/Car_culture "Car culture"), giving California's cities a reputation for severe [traffic congestion](/wiki/Traffic_congestion "Traffic congestion"). Construction and maintenance of state roads and statewide transportation planning are primarily the responsibility of the [California Department of Transportation](/wiki/California_Department_of_Transportation "California Department of Transportation"), nicknamed "Caltrans". The rapidly growing population of the state is straining all of its transportation networks, and California has some of the worst roads in the U.S.[[319]](#cite_note-327)[[320]](#cite_note-328) The [Reason Foundation](/wiki/Reason_Foundation "Reason Foundation")'s 19th Annual Report on the Performance of State Highway Systems ranked California's highways the third-worst of any state, with Alaska second, and Rhode Island first.[[321]](#cite_note-329)

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/San_Francisco_Bay_Ferry_Hydrus_May_2017_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-San_Francisco_Bay_Ferry_Hydrus_May_2017_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:San_Francisco_Bay_Ferry_Hydrus_May_2017_(cropped).jpg)

[San Francisco Bay Ferry](/wiki/San_Francisco_Bay_Ferry "San Francisco Bay Ferry") is a public [water taxi](/wiki/Water_taxi "Water taxi") system in the [Bay Area](/wiki/Bay_Area "Bay Area").

The state has been a pioneer in road construction. One of the state's more visible landmarks, the [Golden Gate Bridge](/wiki/Golden_Gate_Bridge "Golden Gate Bridge"), was the [longest suspension bridge main span](/wiki/List_of_longest_suspension_bridge_spans "List of longest suspension bridge spans") in the world at 4,200 feet (1,300 m) between 1937 (when it opened) and 1964. With its orange paint and panoramic views of the bay, this highway bridge is a popular tourist attraction and also accommodates pedestrians and bicyclists. The [San Francisco–Oakland Bay Bridge](/wiki/San_Francisco%E2%80%93Oakland_Bay_Bridge "San Francisco–Oakland Bay Bridge") (often abbreviated the "Bay Bridge"), completed in 1936, transports about 280,000 vehicles per day on two-decks. Its two sections meet at [Yerba Buena Island](/wiki/Yerba_Buena_Island "Yerba Buena Island") through the world's largest diameter transportation bore tunnel, at 76 feet (23 m) wide by 58 feet (18 m) high.[[322]](#cite_note-330) The [Arroyo Seco Parkway](/wiki/Arroyo_Seco_Parkway "Arroyo Seco Parkway"), connecting Los Angeles and [Pasadena](/wiki/Pasadena,_California "Pasadena, California"), opened in 1940 as the first freeway in the Western U.S.[[323]](#cite_note-331) It was later extended south to the [Four Level Interchange](/wiki/Four_Level_Interchange "Four Level Interchange") in downtown Los Angeles, regarded as the first [stack interchange](/wiki/Stack_interchange "Stack interchange") ever built.[[324]](#cite_note-332)

The [California Highway Patrol](/wiki/California_Highway_Patrol "California Highway Patrol") is the largest statewide police agency in the U.S. in employment with more than 10,000 employees. They are responsible for providing any police-sanctioned service to anyone on California's state-maintained highways and on state property.

By the end of 2021, 30,610,058 people in California held a [California Department of Motor Vehicles](/wiki/California_Department_of_Motor_Vehicles "California Department of Motor Vehicles")-issued [driver's licenses](/wiki/Driver%27s_license "Driver's license") or [state identification card](/wiki/Identity_documents_in_the_United_States "Identity documents in the United States"), and there were 36,229,205 [registered vehicles](/wiki/Vehicle_registration "Vehicle registration"), including 25,643,076 automobiles, 853,368 motorcycles, 8,981,787 trucks and trailers, and 121,716 miscellaneous vehicles (including historical vehicles and farm equipment).[[325]](#cite_note-333)

#### Air travel

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/TheThemeBuildingLosAngeles_%28cropped2%29.jpg/250px-TheThemeBuildingLosAngeles_%28cropped2%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:TheThemeBuildingLosAngeles_(cropped2).jpg)

[Los Angeles Intl. Airport](/wiki/Los_Angeles_International_Airport "Los Angeles International Airport") (LAX) is the [4th busiest airport in the world](/wiki/World%27s_busiest_airports_by_passenger_traffic "World's busiest airports by passenger traffic").

[Los Angeles International Airport](/wiki/Los_Angeles_International_Airport "Los Angeles International Airport") (LAX), [the 4th busiest airport in the world in 2018](/wiki/World%27s_busiest_airports_by_passenger_traffic "World's busiest airports by passenger traffic"), and [San Francisco International Airport](/wiki/San_Francisco_International_Airport "San Francisco International Airport") (SFO), [the 25th busiest airport in the world in 2018](/wiki/World%27s_busiest_airports_by_passenger_traffic "World's busiest airports by passenger traffic"), are major hubs for trans-Pacific and transcontinental traffic. There are about a dozen important commercial airports and many more [general aviation](/wiki/General_aviation "General aviation") [airports](/wiki/List_of_airports_in_California "List of airports in California") throughout the state.

#### Railroads

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Pacific_Surfliner_%40_San_Clemente_CA..jpg/250px-Pacific_Surfliner_%40_San_Clemente_CA..jpg)](/wiki/File:Pacific_Surfliner_@_San_Clemente_CA..jpg)

[Amtrak California](/wiki/Amtrak_California "Amtrak California")'s *[Pacific Surfliner](/wiki/Pacific_Surfliner "Pacific Surfliner")* in [San Clemente](/wiki/San_Clemente,_California "San Clemente, California"), on the [Orange Coast](/wiki/Orange_Coast "Orange Coast")

[Inter-city rail](/wiki/Inter-city_rail "Inter-city rail") travel is provided by [Amtrak California](/wiki/Amtrak_California "Amtrak California"); the three routes, the *[Capitol Corridor](/wiki/Capitol_Corridor "Capitol Corridor")*, *[Pacific Surfliner](/wiki/Pacific_Surfliner "Pacific Surfliner")*, and *[Gold Runner](/wiki/Gold_Runner "Gold Runner")*, are funded by Caltrans. These services are the busiest intercity rail lines in the U.S. outside the [Northeast Corridor](/wiki/Northeast_Corridor "Northeast Corridor") and ridership is continuing to set records. The routes are becoming increasingly popular over flying, especially on the LAX-SFO route.[[326]](#cite_note-334) Integrated [subway](/wiki/Rapid_transit "Rapid transit") and [light rail](/wiki/Light_rail "Light rail") networks are found in Los Angeles ([Los Angeles Metro Rail](/wiki/Los_Angeles_Metro_Rail "Los Angeles Metro Rail")) and San Francisco ([Muni Metro](/wiki/Muni_Metro "Muni Metro")). Light rail systems are also found in San Jose ([VTA light rail](/wiki/VTA_light_rail "VTA light rail")), San Diego ([San Diego Trolley](/wiki/San_Diego_Trolley "San Diego Trolley")), Sacramento ([SacRT light rail](/wiki/SacRT_light_rail "SacRT light rail")), and Northern San Diego County ([Sprinter](/wiki/Sprinter_(rail_service) "Sprinter (rail service)")). Furthermore, [commuter rail](/wiki/Commuter_rail "Commuter rail") networks serve the San Francisco Bay Area ([Altamont Corridor Express](/wiki/Altamont_Corridor_Express "Altamont Corridor Express"), [Bay Area Rapid Transit](/wiki/Bay_Area_Rapid_Transit "Bay Area Rapid Transit"), [Caltrain](/wiki/Caltrain "Caltrain"), [Sonoma–Marin Area Rail Transit](/wiki/Sonoma%E2%80%93Marin_Area_Rail_Transit "Sonoma–Marin Area Rail Transit")), Greater Los Angeles ([Metrolink](/wiki/Metrolink_(California) "Metrolink (California)")), and San Diego County ([Coaster](/wiki/Coaster_(rail_service) "Coaster (rail service)")).

The [California High-Speed Rail Authority](/wiki/California_High-Speed_Rail_Authority "California High-Speed Rail Authority") was authorized in 1996 by the state legislature to plan a [California High-Speed Rail](/wiki/California_High-Speed_Rail "California High-Speed Rail") system to put before the voters. The plan they devised, [2008 California Proposition 1A](/wiki/2008_California_Proposition_1A "2008 California Proposition 1A"), connecting all the major population centers in the state, was approved by the voters at the November 2008 general election.[[327]](#cite_note-335) The first phase of construction was begun in 2015, and the first segment 171 miles (275 km) long, is planned to be put into operation by the end of 2030. Planning and work on the rest of the system is continuing, with funding for completing it is an ongoing issue.[[328]](#cite_note-336) California's 2023 integrated passenger rail master plan includes a high speed rail system.[[329]](#cite_note-337)

#### Busses

Nearly all counties operate [bus](/wiki/Bus "Bus") lines, and many cities operate their own city bus lines as well. Intercity bus travel is provided by [Greyhound](/wiki/Greyhound_Lines "Greyhound Lines"), [Megabus](/wiki/Megabus_(North_America) "Megabus (North America)"), and [Amtrak Thruway](/wiki/Amtrak_Thruway "Amtrak Thruway").

### Water

Main article: [Water in California](/wiki/Water_in_California "Water in California")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Aerial_view_of_Shasta_Dam_and_Shasta_Lake_%282019%29.jpg/250px-Aerial_view_of_Shasta_Dam_and_Shasta_Lake_%282019%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_Shasta_Dam_and_Shasta_Lake_(2019).jpg)

[Lake Shasta](/wiki/Lake_Shasta "Lake Shasta"), in the [Shasta Cascade](/wiki/Shasta_Cascade "Shasta Cascade") region, is California's largest reservoir.

California's interconnected water system is the world's largest, managing over 40,000,000 [acre-feet](/wiki/Acre-foot "Acre-foot") (49 km3) of water per year, centered on six main systems of aqueducts and infrastructure projects.[[330]](#cite_note-338) Water use and conservation in California is a politically divisive issue, as the state experiences periodic droughts and has to balance the demands of its large agricultural and urban sectors, especially in the arid southern portion of the state. The state's widespread redistribution of water also invites the frequent scorn of environmentalists.

The [California Water Wars](/wiki/California_Water_Wars "California Water Wars"), a conflict between Los Angeles and the Owens Valley over water rights, is one of the most well-known examples of the struggle to secure adequate water supplies.[[331]](#cite_note-339) Former California Governor [Arnold Schwarzenegger](/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger "Arnold Schwarzenegger") said:

> We've been in crisis for quite some time because we're now 38 million people and not anymore 18 million people like we were in the late 60s. So it developed into a battle between environmentalists and farmers and between the south and the north and between rural and urban. And everyone has been fighting for the last four decades about water.[[332]](#cite_note-340)

## Government and politics

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Sacramento%2C_California_State_Capitol.jpg/330px-Sacramento%2C_California_State_Capitol.jpg)](/wiki/File:Sacramento,_California_State_Capitol.jpg)

The [California Capitol](/wiki/California_Capitol "California Capitol") in [Sacramento](/wiki/Sacramento,_California "Sacramento, California"), seat of the [California government](/wiki/Government_of_California "Government of California"), hosts the [California Legislature](/wiki/California_Legislature "California Legislature") and the [Governor of California](/wiki/Governor_of_California "Governor of California").

### State government

Main article: [Government of California](/wiki/Government_of_California "Government of California")

The capital city of California is [Sacramento](/wiki/Sacramento,_California "Sacramento, California").[[333]](#cite_note-341) The state is organized into three branches of government: the [executive branch](/wiki/Executive_branch "Executive branch") consisting of the [governor](/wiki/Governor_of_California "Governor of California")[[334]](#cite_note-342) and the other independently elected constitutional officers; the [legislative branch](/wiki/Legislative_branch "Legislative branch") consisting of the [Assembly](/wiki/California_State_Assembly "California State Assembly") and [Senate](/wiki/California_State_Senate "California State Senate");[[335]](#cite_note-343) and the [judicial branch](/wiki/Judicial_branch "Judicial branch") consisting of the [Supreme Court of California](/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_California "Supreme Court of California") and lower courts.[[336]](#cite_note-344) The state also allows [ballot propositions](/wiki/California_ballot_proposition "California ballot proposition"): direct participation of the electorate by [initiative](/wiki/Popular_initiative "Popular initiative"), [referendum](/wiki/Referendum "Referendum"), [recall](/wiki/Recall_election "Recall election"), and [ratification](/wiki/Ratification "Ratification").[[337]](#cite_note-345) Before the passage of [Proposition 14 in 2010](/wiki/California_Proposition_14_(2010) "California Proposition 14 (2010)"), California allowed each political party to choose whether to have a [closed primary](/wiki/Closed_primary "Closed primary") or a primary where only party members and [independents](/wiki/Independent_(politics) "Independent (politics)") vote. After June 8, 2010, when Proposition 14 was approved, excepting only the U.S. president and county central committee offices,[[338]](#cite_note-346) all candidates in the primary elections are listed on the ballot with their preferred party affiliation, but they are not the official nominee of that party.[[339]](#cite_note-DB2-347) At the primary election, the two candidates with the top votes will advance to the general election regardless of party affiliation.[[339]](#cite_note-DB2-347) This is known as a [nonpartisan blanket primary](/wiki/Nonpartisan_blanket_primary "Nonpartisan blanket primary"). If at a special primary election, one candidate receives more than 50% of all the votes cast, they are elected to fill the vacancy and no special general election will be held.[[339]](#cite_note-DB2-347)

#### Executive branch

Main articles: [California executive branch](/wiki/California_executive_branch "California executive branch"), [Governor of California](/wiki/Governor_of_California "Governor of California"), and [List of California state agencies](/wiki/List_of_California_state_agencies "List of California state agencies")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Stanford_Mansion_-_Sacramento%2C_California_%286007570640%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Stanford_Mansion_-_Sacramento%2C_California_%286007570640%29_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Stanford_Mansion_-_Sacramento,_California_(6007570640)_(cropped).jpg)

The [Stanford Mansion](/wiki/Leland_Stanford_Mansion "Leland Stanford Mansion") is the official reception center for the California government, as well as one of the workplaces of the governor.

The California executive branch consists of the governor and seven other elected constitutional officers: [lieutenant governor](/wiki/Lieutenant_Governor_of_California "Lieutenant Governor of California"), [attorney general](/wiki/California_Attorney_General "California Attorney General"), [secretary of state](/wiki/Secretary_of_State_of_California "Secretary of State of California"), [state controller](/wiki/California_State_Controller "California State Controller"), [state treasurer](/wiki/California_State_Treasurer "California State Treasurer"), [insurance commissioner](/wiki/California_Insurance_Commissioner "California Insurance Commissioner"), and [state superintendent of public instruction](/wiki/California_State_Superintendent_of_Public_Instruction "California State Superintendent of Public Instruction"). They serve four-year terms and may be re-elected only once.[[340]](#cite_note-348)

The many California state agencies that are under the governor's cabinet are grouped together to form cabinet-level entities that are referred to by government officials as "superagencies". Those departments that are directly under the other independently elected officers work separately from these superagencies.[[341]](#cite_note-349)[[342]](#cite_note-Lawrence-350)

#### Legislative branch

Main article: [California State Legislature](/wiki/California_State_Legislature "California State Legislature")

The California State Legislature consists of a 40-member Senate and 80-member Assembly.[[343]](#cite_note-leg_members_and_term_limits_first_m_dec-351) Senators serve four-year terms and Assembly members two. Members of the Assembly are subject to [term limits](/wiki/Term_limit "Term limit") of six terms, and members of the Senate are subject to term limits of three terms.

#### Judicial branch

Main article: [Judiciary of California](/wiki/Judiciary_of_California "Judiciary of California")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Supremecourtofcaliforniamaincourthouse_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Supremecourtofcaliforniamaincourthouse_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Supremecourtofcaliforniamaincourthouse_(cropped).jpg)

The [Supreme Court of California](/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_California "Supreme Court of California") primarily convenes at the [Earl Warren Building](/wiki/Earl_Warren_Building "Earl Warren Building") in San Francisco (pictured), but also holds session in Sacramento and Los Angeles.

California's legal system is explicitly based upon English [common law](/wiki/Common_law "Common law")[[344]](#cite_note-Cal_Civil_Code_22.2-352) but carries many features from Spanish [civil law](/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system) "Civil law (legal system)"), such as [community property](/wiki/Community_property "Community property"). California's prison population grew from 25,000 in 1980 to over 170,000 in 2007.[[345]](#cite_note-Thompson_2007-353) [Capital punishment](/wiki/Capital_punishment_in_California "Capital punishment in California") is a legal form of punishment and the state has the largest "[Death Row](/wiki/Death_Row "Death Row")" population in the country (though Oklahoma and Texas are far more active in carrying out executions).[[346]](#cite_note-354)[[347]](#cite_note-355) California has performed [13 executions](/wiki/List_of_people_executed_in_California "List of people executed in California") since 1976, with the last being in 2006.[[348]](#cite_note-356)

California's judiciary system is the largest in the U.S. with a total of 1,600 judges (the federal system has only about 840). At the apex is the seven-member Supreme Court of California, while the [California Courts of Appeal](/wiki/California_Courts_of_Appeal "California Courts of Appeal") serve as the primary [appellate courts](/wiki/Appellate_court "Appellate court") and the [California Superior Courts](/wiki/California_Superior_Courts "California Superior Courts") serve as the primary [trial courts](/wiki/Trial_court "Trial court"). [Justices of the Supreme Court](/wiki/List_of_justices_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_California "List of justices of the Supreme Court of California") and Courts of Appeal are appointed by the governor, but are subject to retention by the electorate every 12 years.

The administration of the state's court system is controlled by the [Judicial Council](/wiki/Judicial_Council_of_California "Judicial Council of California"), composed of the chief justice of the California Supreme Court, 14 judicial officers, four representatives from the [State Bar of California](/wiki/State_Bar_of_California "State Bar of California"), and one member from each house of the state legislature.

In fiscal year 2020–2021, the state judiciary's 2,000 judicial officers and 18,000 judicial branch employees processed approximately 4.4 million cases.[[349]](#cite_note-2022CourtStatisticsReport-357)

### Local government

Main article: [Local government in California](/wiki/Local_government_in_California "Local government in California")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/San_Francisco_City_Hall_September_2013_panorama_3.jpg/250px-San_Francisco_City_Hall_September_2013_panorama_3.jpg)](/wiki/File:San_Francisco_City_Hall_September_2013_panorama_3.jpg)

[San Francisco City Hall](/wiki/San_Francisco_City_Hall "San Francisco City Hall") is the seat of government of the consolidated [City and County of San Francisco](/wiki/Government_of_San_Francisco "Government of San Francisco").

California has an extensive system of local government that manages public functions throughout the state. Like most states, California is divided into [counties](/wiki/County_(United_States) "County (United States)"), of which there are 58 (including [San Francisco](/wiki/San_Francisco "San Francisco")) covering the entire state. Most urbanized areas are incorporated as [cities](/wiki/City "City"). [School districts](/wiki/List_of_school_districts_in_California "List of school districts in California"), which are independent of cities and counties, handle public education. Many other functions, such as fire protection and water supply, especially in [unincorporated areas](/wiki/Unincorporated_area "Unincorporated area"), are handled by [special districts](/wiki/Special-purpose_district "Special-purpose district").

#### Counties

See also: [List of counties in California](/wiki/List_of_counties_in_California "List of counties in California")

California is divided into [58 counties](/wiki/List_of_counties_in_California "List of counties in California"). Per Article 11, Section 1, of the [Constitution of California](/wiki/Constitution_of_California "Constitution of California"), they are the legal subdivisions of the state. The county government provides countywide services such as [law enforcement](/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_California "Law enforcement in California"), [jails](/wiki/List_of_California_county_jails "List of California county jails"), elections and voter registration, vital records, property assessment and records, tax collection, public health, health care, social services, libraries, flood control, fire protection, animal control, agricultural regulations, building inspections, ambulance services, and education departments in charge of maintaining statewide standards.[[350]](#cite_note-358)[[351]](#cite_note-359) In addition, the county serves as the local government for all [unincorporated areas](/wiki/Unincorporated_area "Unincorporated area"). Each county is governed by an elected [board of supervisors](/wiki/Board_of_supervisors "Board of supervisors").[[352]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaldassare199850-360)

#### City and town governments

See also: [List of municipalities in California](/wiki/List_of_municipalities_in_California "List of municipalities in California")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Los_Angeles_City_Hall_01_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Los_Angeles_City_Hall_01_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Los_Angeles_City_Hall_01_(cropped).jpg)

[Los Angeles City Hall](/wiki/Los_Angeles_City_Hall "Los Angeles City Hall"), seat of the [Government of Los Angeles](/wiki/Government_of_Los_Angeles "Government of Los Angeles")

Incorporated cities and towns in California are either [charter](/wiki/Charter_city "Charter city") or general-law municipalities.[[166]](#cite_note-Cal_Gov_Code_34502-168) General-law municipalities owe their existence to state law and are consequently governed by it; charter municipalities are governed by their own city or town charters. Municipalities incorporated in the 19th century tend to be charter municipalities. All ten of the state's most populous cities are charter cities. Most small cities have a [council–manager](/wiki/Council%E2%80%93manager_government "Council–manager government") form of government, where the elected city council appoints a city manager to supervise the operations of the city. Some larger cities have a directly elected mayor who oversees the city government. In many council-manager cities, the city council selects one of its members as a mayor, sometimes rotating through the council membership—but this type of mayoral position is primarily ceremonial. The [Government of San Francisco](/wiki/Government_of_San_Francisco "Government of San Francisco") is the only [consolidated city-county](/wiki/Consolidated_city-county "Consolidated city-county") in California, where both the city and county governments have been merged into one unified jurisdiction.

#### School districts and special districts

See also: [List of school districts in California](/wiki/List_of_school_districts_in_California "List of school districts in California")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/LAUSD_HQ_Bldg.jpg/250px-LAUSD_HQ_Bldg.jpg)](/wiki/File:LAUSD_HQ_Bldg.jpg)

The [Los Angeles Unified School District](/wiki/Los_Angeles_Unified_School_District "Los Angeles Unified School District") is the [second largest in the U.S. by enrollment](/wiki/List_of_the_largest_school_districts_in_the_United_States_by_enrollment "List of the largest school districts in the United States by enrollment").

About 1,102 [school districts](/wiki/School_district "School district"), independent of cities and counties, handle California's [public education](/wiki/Public_education "Public education").[[353]](#cite_note-census2012p25-361) California school districts may be organized as elementary districts, high school districts, unified school districts combining elementary and high school grades, or community college districts.[[353]](#cite_note-census2012p25-361)

There are about 3,400 [special districts](/wiki/Special_district_(United_States) "Special district (United States)") in California.[[354]](#cite_note-362) A special district, defined by California Government Code § 16271(d) as "any agency of the state for the local performance of governmental or proprietary functions within limited boundaries", provides a limited range of services within a defined geographic area. The geographic area of a special district can spread across multiple cities or counties, or could consist of only a portion of one. Most of California's special districts are *single-purpose districts*, and provide one service.

### Federal representation

Main article: [United States congressional delegations from California](/wiki/United_States_congressional_delegations_from_California "United States congressional delegations from California")

See also: [California's congressional districts](/wiki/California%27s_congressional_districts "California's congressional districts")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/California_Congressional_Delegation_honoring_Pete_Stark.jpg/250px-California_Congressional_Delegation_honoring_Pete_Stark.jpg)](/wiki/File:California_Congressional_Delegation_honoring_Pete_Stark.jpg)

Members of the [California Congressional Delegation](/wiki/United_States_congressional_delegations_from_California "United States congressional delegations from California") in 2020

The state of California sends [52](/wiki/California%27s_congressional_districts "California's congressional districts") members to the [House of Representatives](/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives "United States House of Representatives"),[[355]](#cite_note-363) the nation's largest congressional state delegation. Consequently, California also has the largest number of [electoral votes](/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States) "Electoral College (United States)") in national presidential elections, with 54. The former [speaker of the House of Representatives](/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives "Speaker of the United States House of Representatives") is the representative of California's 20th district, [Kevin McCarthy](/wiki/Kevin_McCarthy "Kevin McCarthy").[[356]](#cite_note-govtrack.us-364)

California is represented in the [U.S. Senate](/wiki/U.S._Senate "U.S. Senate") by [Alex Padilla](/wiki/Alex_Padilla "Alex Padilla") and [Adam Schiff](/wiki/Adam_Schiff "Adam Schiff").

In August 2025 Governor Newsom secured legislative approval of [California Proposition 50](/wiki/2025_California_Proposition_50 "2025 California Proposition 50"). In November 2025 voters will vote on a state constitutional amendment that will allow redistricting of the California map. The goal is to switch five seats from Republican to Democratic control. It is a response to the decision of Texas to redistrict its Congressional. map to add five new Republican seats.[[357]](#cite_note-365)[[358]](#cite_note-366)[[359]](#cite_note-367)

### Armed forces

See also: [California during World War II](/wiki/California_during_World_War_II "California during World War II")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN-70%29%2C_USS_Ronald_Reagan_%28CVN-76%29_and_USS_John_C._Stennis_%28CVN-74%29_at_NAS_North_Island_in_June_2015_%28cropped%29.JPG/250px-USS_Carl_Vinson_%28CVN-70%29%2C_USS_Ronald_Reagan_%28CVN-76%29_and_USS_John_C._Stennis_%28CVN-74%29_at_NAS_North_Island_in_June_2015_%28cropped%29.JPG)](/wiki/File:USS_Carl_Vinson_(CVN-70),_USS_Ronald_Reagan_(CVN-76)_and_USS_John_C._Stennis_(CVN-74)_at_NAS_North_Island_in_June_2015_(cropped).JPG)

View of [NAS North Island](/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_North_Island "Naval Air Station North Island") at [Naval Base Coronado](/wiki/Naval_Base_Coronado "Naval Base Coronado") in [San Diego](/wiki/San_Diego "San Diego")

In California, as of 2009[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=California&action=edit), the [U.S. Department of Defense](/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Defense "U.S. Department of Defense") had a total of 117,806 [active duty](/wiki/Active_duty "Active duty") servicemembers of which 88,370 were [Sailors](/wiki/United_States_Navy "United States Navy") or [Marines](/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps "United States Marine Corps"), 18,339 were [Airmen](/wiki/United_States_Air_Force "United States Air Force"), and 11,097 were [Soldiers](/wiki/United_States_Army "United States Army"), with 61,365 Department of Defense civilian employees. Additionally, there were a total of 57,792 [Reservists and Guardsman](/wiki/Reserve_components_of_the_United_States_Armed_Forces "Reserve components of the United States Armed Forces") in California.[[360]](#cite_note-368)

In 2010, [Los Angeles County](/wiki/Los_Angeles_County "Los Angeles County") was the largest origin of military recruits in the U.S. by county, with 1,437 individuals enlisting in the military.[[361]](#cite_note-369) However, as of 2002[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=California&action=edit), Californians were relatively under-represented in the military as a proportion to its population.[[362]](#cite_note-370)

In 2000, California, had 2,569,340 veterans of U.S. [military service](/wiki/Military_service "Military service").[[363]](#cite_note-City_Data_CA_Armed_Forces-371) As of 2010[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=California&action=edit), there were 1,942,775 veterans living in California, of which 1,457,875 served during a period of armed conflict, and just over four thousand served [before World War II](/wiki/Interwar_period "Interwar period") (the largest population of this group of any state).[[364]](#cite_note-372)

California's military forces consist of the [Army and Air National Guard](/wiki/California_National_Guard "California National Guard"), the [naval and state military reserve](/wiki/California_State_Military_Reserve "California State Military Reserve") (militia), and the [California Cadet Corps](/wiki/California_Cadet_Corps "California Cadet Corps").

### Politics

Main articles: [Politics of California](/wiki/Politics_of_California "Politics of California") and [Elections in California](/wiki/Elections_in_California "Elections in California")

See also: [Politics of California before 1900](/wiki/Politics_of_California_before_1900 "Politics of California before 1900") and [Political party strength in California](/wiki/Political_party_strength_in_California "Political party strength in California")

California registered voters as of February 10, 2025[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=California&action=edit)[[365]](#cite_note-373)

|  | [No Party Preference](/wiki/Independent_politician_(United_States) "Independent politician (United States)") | 5,116,983 | 22.34% |
|  | [American Independent](/wiki/American_Independent_Party "American Independent Party") | 896,260 | 3.91% |
|  | [Libertarian](/wiki/Libertarian_Party_of_California "Libertarian Party of California") | 233,052 | 1.02% |
|  | [Peace and Freedom](/wiki/Peace_and_Freedom_Party "Peace and Freedom Party") | 141,785 | 0.62% |
|  | [Green](/wiki/Green_Party_of_California "Green Party of California") | 110,649 | 0.48% |
| Other | | 258,490 | 1.13% |
| Total | | 22,900,896 | 100% |

California has an idiosyncratic political culture compared to the rest of the country, and is sometimes regarded as a trendsetter.[[366]](#cite_note-374) In socio-cultural mores and national politics, Californians are perceived as more [liberal](/wiki/Modern_liberalism_in_the_United_States "Modern liberalism in the United States") than other Americans, especially those who live in the inland states. In the [2016 U.S. presidential election](/wiki/2016_U.S._presidential_election "2016 U.S. presidential election"), California had the third highest percentage of Democratic votes behind the [District of Columbia](/wiki/District_of_Columbia "District of Columbia") and [Hawaii](/wiki/Hawaii "Hawaii").[[367]](#cite_note-375) In the [2020 U.S. presidential election](/wiki/2020_U.S._presidential_election "2020 U.S. presidential election"), it had the 6th highest behind D.C., [Vermont](/wiki/Vermont "Vermont"), [Massachusetts](/wiki/Massachusetts "Massachusetts"), [Maryland](/wiki/Maryland "Maryland"), and Hawaii. According to the Cook Political Report, California contains five of the 15 most Democratic congressional districts in the U.S.

California was the second state [to recall their state governor](/wiki/California_gubernatorial_recall_election,_2003 "California gubernatorial recall election, 2003"), the second state to legalize abortion, and the only state to ban marriage for gay couples twice by vote (including [Proposition 8](/wiki/California_Proposition_8 "California Proposition 8") in 2008). Voters also passed [Proposition 71 in 2004](/wiki/California_Proposition_71_(2004) "California Proposition 71 (2004)") to fund [stem cell](/wiki/Stem_cell "Stem cell") research, making California the [second state to legalize stem cell research](/wiki/Stem_cell_laws_and_policy_in_the_United_States#Legalization_and_funding "Stem cell laws and policy in the United States"), and [Proposition 14 in 2010](/wiki/California_Proposition_14_(2010) "California Proposition 14 (2010)") to completely change the state's [primary election](/wiki/Partisan_primary "Partisan primary") process. California has also experienced [disputes over water rights](/wiki/California_Water_Wars "California Water Wars"); and a [tax revolt](/wiki/Tax_revolt "Tax revolt"), culminating with the passage of [Proposition 13 in 1978](/wiki/California_Proposition_13_(1978) "California Proposition 13 (1978)"), limiting state [property taxes](/wiki/Property_tax "Property tax"). California voters have rejected [affirmative action](/wiki/Affirmative_action "Affirmative action") on multiple occasions, most recently in November 2020.

The state's trend towards the [Democratic Party](/wiki/California_Democratic_Party "California Democratic Party") and away from the [Republican Party](/wiki/California_Republican_Party "California Republican Party") can be seen in state elections. From 1899 to 1939, California had exclusively Republican governors. Since 1990, California has generally elected Democratic candidates to federal, state and local offices, including current Governor [Gavin Newsom](/wiki/Gavin_Newsom "Gavin Newsom"); however, the state has elected Republican Governors, though many of its Republican Governors, such as Arnold Schwarzenegger, tend to be considered moderate Republicans and more [centrist](/wiki/Centrism "Centrism") than the national party.

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Code_Pink_and_pro-Marine_protesters.jpg/250px-Code_Pink_and_pro-Marine_protesters.jpg)](/wiki/File:Code_Pink_and_pro-Marine_protesters.jpg)

[Anti-war](/wiki/Anti-war_movement "Anti-war movement") protesters and pro-military counterprotesters in [Berkeley](/wiki/Berkeley,_California "Berkeley, California") (2008)

Several political movements have advocated for [California independence](/wiki/Partition_and_secession_in_California "Partition and secession in California"). The [California National Party](/wiki/California_National_Party "California National Party") and the [California Freedom Coalition](/wiki/California_Freedom_Coalition "California Freedom Coalition") both advocate for California independence along the lines of [progressivism](/wiki/Progressivism_in_the_United_States "Progressivism in the United States") and [civic nationalism](/wiki/Civic_nationalism "Civic nationalism").[[368]](#cite_note-376) The [Yes California](/wiki/Yes_California "Yes California") movement attempted to organize an independence referendum via [ballot initiative](/wiki/Ballot_initiative "Ballot initiative") for 2019, which was then postponed.[[369]](#cite_note-377)

The Democrats also hold a supermajority in both houses of the state legislature. There are 62 Democrats and 18 Republicans in the Assembly; and 32 Democrats and eight Republicans in the Senate.

From [1952](/wiki/1952_United_States_presidential_election "1952 United States presidential election") through [1988](/wiki/1988_United_States_presidential_election "1988 United States presidential election"), California was a Republican-leaning state, as the party carried the state's electoral votes in nine of ten elections, with [1964](/wiki/1964_United_States_presidential_election "1964 United States presidential election") as the sole exception. Southern California Republicans [Richard Nixon](/wiki/Richard_Nixon "Richard Nixon") and [Ronald Reagan](/wiki/Ronald_Reagan "Ronald Reagan") were both elected twice as the 37th and 40th U.S. Presidents, respectively. However, Democrats have won all of California's electoral votes for the last eight elections, starting in [1992](/wiki/1992_United_States_presidential_election "1992 United States presidential election").

In the U.S. House, the Democrats held a 34–19 edge in the California delegation of the [110th U.S. Congress](/wiki/110th_U.S._Congress "110th U.S. Congress") in 2007. As the result of [gerrymandering](/wiki/Gerrymandering "Gerrymandering"), the districts in California were usually dominated by one or the other party, and few districts were considered competitive. In 2008, Californians passed Proposition 20 to empower a 14-member independent citizen commission to redraw districts for both local politicians and Congress. After the 2012 elections, when the new system took effect, Democrats gained four seats and held a 38–15 majority in the delegation. Following the [2018 midterm House elections](/wiki/2018_United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections "2018 United States House of Representatives elections"), Democrats won 46 out of 53 congressional house seats in California, leaving Republicans with seven.

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Protest_at_Los_Angeles_City_Hall_%286248466227%29.jpg/250px-Protest_at_Los_Angeles_City_Hall_%286248466227%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Protest_at_Los_Angeles_City_Hall_(6248466227).jpg)

[Occupy Los Angeles](/wiki/Occupy_Los_Angeles "Occupy Los Angeles") movement (2011)

In general, Democratic strength is centered in the populous [coastal regions](/wiki/Coastal_California "Coastal California") of the [Los Angeles metropolitan area](/wiki/Los_Angeles_metropolitan_area "Los Angeles metropolitan area") and the [San Francisco Bay Area](/wiki/San_Francisco_Bay_Area "San Francisco Bay Area"). Republican strength is still greatest in eastern parts of the state. [Orange County](/wiki/Orange_County,_California "Orange County, California") had remained largely Republican until the 2016 and 2018 elections, in which a majority of the county's votes were cast for Democratic candidates.[[370]](#cite_note-378)[[371]](#cite_note-379) One study ranked [Berkeley](/wiki/Berkeley_California "Berkeley California"), [Oakland](/wiki/Oakland,_California "Oakland, California"), [Inglewood](/wiki/Inglewood,_California "Inglewood, California") and [San Francisco](/wiki/San_Francisco "San Francisco") in the top 20 most liberal American cities; and [Bakersfield](/wiki/Bakersfield,_California "Bakersfield, California"), [Orange](/wiki/Orange,_California "Orange, California"), [Escondido](/wiki/Escondido,_California "Escondido, California"), [Garden Grove](/wiki/Garden_Grove,_California "Garden Grove, California"), and [Simi Valley](/wiki/Simi_Valley,_California "Simi Valley, California") in the top 20 most conservative cities.[[372]](#cite_note-380)

In October 2022, out of the 26,876,800 people eligible to vote, 21,940,274 people were registered to vote.[[373]](#cite_note-SoS2022GenVotRegStats-381) Of the people registered, the three largest registered groups were Democrats (10,283,258), Republicans (5,232,094), and [No Party Preference](/wiki/Independent_voter "Independent voter") (4,943,696).[[373]](#cite_note-SoS2022GenVotRegStats-381)

California retains the [death penalty](/wiki/Death_penalty_in_California "Death penalty in California"), though it has not been used since 2006.[[374]](#cite_note-382)

### Foreign relations

California is a member of the [Commission of the Californias](/wiki/Commission_of_the_Californias "Commission of the Californias"), a tri-lateral forum for cooperation between the U.S. state of California and the Mexican states of [Baja California](/wiki/Baja_California "Baja California") and [Baja California Sur](/wiki/Baja_California_Sur "Baja California Sur").[[375]](#cite_note-Memorandum-383)

Twinned regions

California has [region twinning arrangements](/wiki/City_twinning "City twinning") with:

* [![Catalonia](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Flag_of_Catalonia.svg/40px-Flag_of_Catalonia.svg.png)](/wiki/Catalonia "Catalonia") [Catalonia](/wiki/Catalonia "Catalonia"), autonomous community of [![Spain](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9a/Flag_of_Spain.svg/40px-Flag_of_Spain.svg.png)](/wiki/Spain "Spain") [Spain](/wiki/Spain "Spain")[[376]](#cite_note-384)
* [![Alberta](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Flag_of_Alberta.svg/40px-Flag_of_Alberta.svg.png)](/wiki/Alberta "Alberta") [Alberta](/wiki/Alberta "Alberta"), province of [![Canada](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Canada_%28Pantone%29.svg/40px-Flag_of_Canada_%28Pantone%29.svg.png)](/wiki/Canada "Canada") [Canada](/wiki/Canada "Canada")[[377]](#cite_note-Soir.senate.ca.gov-385)[[378]](#cite_note-386)
* [![Jeju Province](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Flag_of_Jeju_Province.svg/40px-Flag_of_Jeju_Province.svg.png)](/wiki/Jeju_Province "Jeju Province") [Jeju Province](/wiki/Jeju_Province "Jeju Province") of [![South Korea](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_South_Korea.svg/40px-Flag_of_South_Korea.svg.png)](/wiki/South_Korea "South Korea") [South Korea](/wiki/South_Korea "South Korea")[[379]](#cite_note-387)
* [![China](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg/40px-Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg.png)](/wiki/China "China") [Guangdong](/wiki/Guangdong "Guangdong"), province of [![China](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg/40px-Flag_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China.svg.png)](/wiki/China "China") [China](/wiki/China "China")[[380]](#cite_note-388)

Following the collapse of the Soviet Union and the end of the Cold War, the Department of Defense (DOD) sharply curtailed procurement spending. In 1995, DOD spending fell below $50 billion for the first time since 1982. Nowhere in the country were the changes in Pentagon outlays more apparent than in Southern California."
